Calibration and Sensitivity: The sensor is exposed to known concentrations of quinine, and the electrochemical response (such as current or potential change) is measured. A calibration curve is constructed to determine the sensor's sensitivity and linear range.\n\n2. Selectivity: The sensor's response to quinine is compared to its response to potential interfering substances (e.g., structurally similar alkaloids or common ions) to ensure that the IDA mechanism is selective for quinine.\n\n3. Reproducibility and Stability: Multiple measurements are performed under identical conditions to assess the reproducibility of the sensor's response. Long-term stability tests may also be conducted to ensure consistent performance over time.\n\n4. Limit of Detection (LOD) and Limit of Quantification (LOQ): The lowest concentration of quinine that can be reliably detected and quantified is determined.\n\n5. Real Sample Analysis: The sensor is tested with real samples (such as tonic water or pharmaceutical formulations) spiked with known amounts of quinine to evaluate its practical applicability and recovery rates.\n\n6. Comparison with Reference Methods: Results from the electrochemical IDA sensor are compared with those obtained from established analytical techniques (e.g., HPLC, spectrophotometry) to validate accuracy.\n\nThe IDA mechanism involves a competitive binding event: a receptor on the sensor surface initially binds an indicator molecule, producing a measurable electrochemical signal. When quinine is introduced, it displaces the indicator due to higher affinity, resulting in a change in the electrochemical signal.
